<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Mario Vargas Llosa</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>

<p><b>Jorge Mario Pedro Vargas Llosa, 1st Marquis of Vargas Llosa</b> (born March 28, 1936), more commonly known as <b>Mario Vargas Llosa</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ɑːr/ 'ar' in 'far'">ɑːr</span><span title="'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span></span> <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/j/ 'y' in 'yes'">j</span><span title="/oʊ/ long 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ə/ 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> <small>Spanish: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Spanish" title="Help:IPA for Spanish">[ˈmaɾjo ˈβaɾgas ˈʎosa]</a></span>), is a Peruvian writer, politician, journalist, essayist, college professor, and recipient of the 2010 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel Prize in Literature</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> Vargas Llosa is one of Latin America's most significant novelists and essayists, and one of the leading writers of his generation. Some critics consider him to have had a larger international impact and worldwide audience than any other writer of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_American_Boom" title="Latin American Boom">Latin American Boom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> Upon announcing the 2010 Nobel Prize in Literature, the <a href="/wiki/Swedish_Academy" title="Swedish Academy">Swedish Academy</a> said it had been given to Vargas Llosa "for his cartography of structures of power and his trenchant images of the individual's resistance, revolt, and defeat".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vargas Llosa rose to fame in the 1960s with novels such as <i><a href="/wiki/The_Time_of_the_Hero" title="The Time of the Hero">The Time of the Hero</a></i> (<i>La ciudad y los perros</i>, literally <i>The City and the Dogs</i>, 1963/1966<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>), <i><a href="/wiki/The_Green_House" title="The Green House">The Green House</a></i> (<i>La casa verde</i>, 1965/1968), and the monumental <i><a href="/wiki/Conversation_in_the_Cathedral" title="Conversation in the Cathedral">Conversation in the Cathedral</a></i> (<i>Conversación en la catedral</i>, 1969/1975). He writes prolifically across an array of <a href="/wiki/Literary_genre" title="Literary genre">literary genres</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Literary_criticism" title="Literary criticism">literary criticism</a> and journalism. His novels include comedies, murder mysteries, historical novels, and political thrillers. Several, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Captain_Pantoja_and_the_Special_Service" title="Captain Pantoja and the Special Service">Captain Pantoja and the Special Service</a></i> (1973/1978) and <i><a href="/wiki/Aunt_Julia_and_the_Scriptwriter" title="Aunt Julia and the Scriptwriter">Aunt Julia and the Scriptwriter</a></i> (1977/1982), have been adapted as feature films.</p>
<p>Many of Vargas Llosa's works are influenced by the writer's perception of <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peruvian</a> society and his own experiences as a native Peruvian. Increasingly- however- he has expanded his range, and tackled themes that arise from other parts of the world. In his essays, Vargas Llosa has made many criticisms of nationalism in different parts of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kordi_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kordi-8">[8]</a></sup> Another change over the course of his career has been a shift from a style and approach associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modernist_literature" title="Modernist literature">literary modernism</a>, to a sometimes playful <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">postmodernism</a>.</p>
<p>Like many Latin American writers, Vargas Llosa has been politically active throughout his career; over the course of his life, he has gradually moved from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Political_left" title="Political left">political left</a> towards <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberalism</a>. While he initially supported the Cuban revolutionary government of <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>, Vargas Llosa later became disenchanted with his policies. He ran for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Peru" title="List of Presidents of Peru">Peruvian presidency</a> in 1990 with the center-right <i><a href="/wiki/Democratic_Front_(Peru)" title="Democratic Front (Peru)">Frente Democrático</a></i> coalition, advocating <a href="/wiki/Classical_liberalism" title="Classical liberalism">classical liberal</a> reforms, but lost the election to <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Fujimori" title="Alberto Fujimori">Alberto Fujimori</a>. He is the person who, in 1990, "coined the phrase that circled the globe",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-terra_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-terra-9">[9]</a></sup> declaring on Mexican television, "Mexico is the perfect dictatorship", a statement which became an adage during the following decade.</p>
<p>In 1995, he wrote and published a children's book called, <i>Hitos y Mitos Literarios</i> (English version as "The Milestones and the Stories of Greatest Literary Works"), which is illustrated by <a href="/wiki/Willi_Glasauer" title="Willi Glasauer">Willi Glasauer</a>, and published by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=C%C3%ADrculo_de_Lectores&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Círculo de Lectores (page does not exist)">Círculo de Lectores</a>. This children's book includes fun facts, trivia, and information accompanied by photos and <a href="/wiki/Willi_Glasauer" title="Willi Glasauer">Willi Glasauer</a>'s illustrations of the likes of <i><a href="/wiki/Brave_New_World" title="Brave New World">Brave New World</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Aldous_Huxley" title="Aldous Huxley">Aldous Huxley</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Transfer_(novel)" title="Manhattan Transfer (novel)">Manhattan Transfer</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/John_Dos_Passos" title="John Dos Passos">John Dos Passos</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Stranger_(novel)" title="The Stranger (novel)">The Stranger</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Albert_Camus" title="Albert Camus">Albert Camus</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lolita" title="Lolita">Lolita</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Nabokov" title="Vladimir Nabokov">Vladimir Nabokov</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/One_Day_in_the_Life_of_Ivan_Denisovich" title="One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich">One Day in the Life of Ivan Denisovich</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Aleksandr_Solzhenitsyn" title="Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn">Aleksandr Solzhenitsyn</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Death_in_Venice" title="Death in Venice">Death in Venice</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Mann" title="Thomas Mann">Thomas Mann</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Great_Gatsby" title="The Great Gatsby">The Great Gatsby</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/F._Scott_Fitzgerald" title="F. Scott Fitzgerald">F. Scott Fitzgerald</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Herzog" title="Herzog">Herzog</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Saul_Bellow" title="Saul Bellow">Saul Bellow</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/East_of_Eden_(novel)" title="East of Eden (novel)">East of Eden</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/John_Steinbeck" title="John Steinbeck">John Steinbeck</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Steppenwolf_(novel)" title="Steppenwolf (novel)">Steppenwolf</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Hermann_Hesse" title="Hermann Hesse">Hermann Hesse</a>, and <i><a href="/wiki/A_Moveable_Feast" title="A Moveable Feast">A Moveable Feast</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Hemingway" title="Ernest Hemingway">Ernest Hemingway</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>
<div class="toc" id="toc">
<div id="toctitle">
<h2>Contents</h2>
</div>
<ul>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_family">Early life and family</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mario_Vargas_Llosa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life and family">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mario Vargas Llosa was born to a middle-class family<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> on March 28, 1936, in the <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peruvian</a> provincial city of <a href="/wiki/Arequipa" title="Arequipa">Arequipa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams17_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams17-12">[12]</a></sup> He was the only child of Ernesto Vargas Maldonado and Dora Llosa Ureta (the former a radio operator in an aviation company, the latter the daughter of an old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Criollo_(people)" title="Criollo (people)">criollo</a> family), who separated a few months before his birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams17_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams17-12">[12]</a></sup> Shortly after Mario's birth, his father revealed that he was having an affair with a German woman; consequently, Mario has two younger half-brothers: Enrique and Ernesto Vargas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mario_vargas_llosa_tesis_universidad_de_san_marcos_1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="801" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Mario_vargas_llosa_tesis_universidad_de_san_marcos_1.jpg/180px-Mario_vargas_llosa_tesis_universidad_de_san_marcos_1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Mario_vargas_llosa_tesis_universidad_de_san_marcos_1.jpg/270px-Mario_vargas_llosa_tesis_universidad_de_san_marcos_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Mario_vargas_llosa_tesis_universidad_de_san_marcos_1.jpg/360px-Mario_vargas_llosa_tesis_universidad_de_san_marcos_1.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mario_vargas_llosa_tesis_universidad_de_san_marcos_1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Mario Vargas Llosa's <a href="/wiki/Thesis" title="Thesis">thesis</a> «Bases para una interpretación de <a href="/wiki/Rub%C3%A9n_Dar%C3%ADo" title="Rubén Darío">Rubén Darío</a>», presented to his <i>alma mater</i>, the <a href="/wiki/National_University_of_San_Marcos" title="National University of San Marcos">National University of San Marcos</a> (<a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>), in 1958.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Vargas Llosa lived with his maternal family in Arequipa until a year after his parents' divorce, when his maternal grandfather was named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Honorary_consul" title="Honorary consul">honorary consul</a> for Peru in <a href="/wiki/Bolivia" title="Bolivia">Bolivia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams17_12-2"><a href="#cite_note-Williams17-12">[12]</a></sup> With his mother and her family, Vargas Llosa then moved to <a href="/wiki/Cochabamba" title="Cochabamba">Cochabamba</a>, Bolivia, where he spent the early years of his childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams17_12-3"><a href="#cite_note-Williams17-12">[12]</a></sup> His maternal family, the Llosas, were sustained by his grandfather, who managed a cotton farm.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> As a child, Vargas Llosa was led to believe that his father had died—his mother and her family did not want to explain that his parents had separated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams24_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams24-15">[15]</a></sup> During the government of Peruvian President <a href="/wiki/Jos%C3%A9_Bustamante_y_Rivero" title="José Bustamante y Rivero">José Bustamante y Rivero</a>, Vargas Llosa's maternal grandfather obtained a diplomatic post in the Peruvian coastal city of <a href="/wiki/Piura" title="Piura">Piura</a> and the entire family returned to Peru.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams24_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams24-15">[15]</a></sup> While in Piura, Vargas Llosa attended elementary school at the religious academy <i>Colegio <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salesian" title="Salesian">Salesiano</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams30_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams30-16">[16]</a></sup> In 1946, at the age of ten, he moved to <a href="/wiki/Lima" title="Lima">Lima</a> and met his father for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams30_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams30-16">[16]</a></sup> His parents re-established their relationship and lived in <a href="/wiki/Magdalena_del_Mar" title="Magdalena del Mar">Magdalena del Mar</a>, a middle-class Lima suburb, during his teenage years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> While in Lima, he studied at the <i>Colegio La Salle</i>, a Christian middle school, from 1947 to 1949.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>When Vargas Llosa was fourteen, his father sent him to the <a href="/wiki/Leoncio_Prado_Military_Academy" title="Leoncio Prado Military Academy">Leoncio Prado Military Academy</a> in Lima.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> At the age of 16, before his graduation, Vargas Llosa began working as an amateur journalist for local newspapers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro9_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castro9-20">[20]</a></sup> He withdrew from the military academy and finished his studies in Piura, where he worked for the local newspaper, <i>La Industria</i>, and witnessed the theatrical performance of his first dramatic work, <i>La huida del Inca</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1953, during the government of <a href="/wiki/Manuel_A._Odr%C3%ADa" title="Manuel A. Odría">Manuel A. Odría</a>, Vargas Llosa enrolled in Lima's <a href="/wiki/National_University_of_San_Marcos" title="National University of San Marcos">National University of San Marcos</a>, to study law and literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> He married <a href="/wiki/Julia_Urquidi" title="Julia Urquidi">Julia Urquidi</a>, his maternal uncle's sister-in-law, in 1955 at the age of 19; she was 10 years older.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro9_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castro9-20">[20]</a></sup> Vargas Llosa began his literary career in earnest in 1957 with the publication of his first short stories, "The Leaders" ("Los jefes") and "The Grandfather" ("El abuelo"), while working for two Peruvian newspapers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Upon his graduation from the National University of San Marcos in 1958, he received a scholarship to study at the <a href="/wiki/Complutense_University_of_Madrid" title="Complutense University of Madrid">Complutense University of Madrid</a> in Spain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In 1960, after his scholarship in Madrid had expired, Vargas Llosa moved to France under the impression that he would receive a scholarship to study there; however, upon arriving in Paris, he learned that his scholarship request was denied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams45_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams45-25">[25]</a></sup> Despite Mario and Julia's unexpected financial status, the couple decided to remain in Paris where he began to write prolifically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams45_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams45-25">[25]</a></sup> Their marriage lasted only a few more years, ending in divorce in 1964.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams54_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams54-26">[26]</a></sup> A year later, Vargas Llosa <a href="/wiki/Cousin_marriage" title="Cousin marriage">married his first cousin</a>, Patricia Llosa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams54_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams54-26">[26]</a></sup> with whom he had three children: <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_Vargas_Llosa" title="Álvaro Vargas Llosa">Álvaro Vargas Llosa</a> (born 1966), a writer and editor; Gonzalo (born 1967), a businessman; and Morgana (born 1974), a photographer. As of 2015, he is in a relationship with Filipina Spanish socialite and TV personality <a href="/wiki/Isabel_Preysler" title="Isabel Preysler">Isabel Preysler</a> and seeking a divorce from Patricia Llosa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-elpais17nov_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-elpais17nov-27">[27]</a></sup> He is an atheist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p><br/></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_career">Writing career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mario_Vargas_Llosa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Writing career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Beginning_and_first_major_works">Beginning and first major works</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mario_Vargas_Llosa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Beginning and first major works">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Vargas Llosa's first novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Time_of_the_Hero" title="The Time of the Hero">The Time of the Hero</a></i> (<i>La ciudad y los perros</i>), was published in 1963. The book is set among a community of cadets in a Lima military school, and the plot is based on the author's own experiences at Lima's <a href="/wiki/Leoncio_Prado_Military_Academy" title="Leoncio Prado Military Academy">Leoncio Prado Military Academy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal32_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal32-30">[30]</a></sup> This early piece gained wide public attention and immediate success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cevallos273_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cevallos273-31">[31]</a></sup> Its vitality and adept use of sophisticated literary techniques immediately impressed critics,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal33_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal33-32">[32]</a></sup> and it won the <i><a href="/wiki/Premio_de_la_Cr%C3%ADtica_Espa%C3%B1ola" title="Premio de la Crítica Española">Premio de la Crítica Española</a></i> award.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cevallos273_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cevallos273-31">[31]</a></sup> Nevertheless, its sharp criticism of the Peruvian military establishment led to controversy in Peru. Several Peruvian generals attacked the novel, claiming that it was the work of a "degenerate mind" and stating that Vargas Llosa was "paid by Ecuador" to undermine the prestige of the Peruvian Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cevallos273_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cevallos273-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1965, Vargas Llosa published his second novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Green_House" title="The Green House">The Green House</a></i> (<i>La casa verde</i>), about a brothel called "The Green House" and how its quasi-mythical presence affects the lives of the characters. The main plot follows Bonifacia, a girl who is about to receive the vows of the church, and her transformation into <i>la Selvatica</i>, the best-known prostitute of "The Green House". The novel was immediately acclaimed, confirming Vargas Llosa as an important voice of Latin American narrative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal_xi_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal_xi-33">[33]</a></sup> <i>The Green House</i> won the first edition of the <a href="/wiki/R%C3%B3mulo_Gallegos_Prize" title="Rómulo Gallegos Prize">Rómulo Gallegos International Novel Prize</a> in 1967, contending with works by veteran <a href="/wiki/Uruguay" title="Uruguay">Uruguayan</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Juan_Carlos_Onetti" title="Juan Carlos Onetti">Juan Carlos Onetti</a> and by <a href="/wiki/Gabriel_Garc%C3%ADa_M%C3%A1rquez" title="Gabriel García Márquez">Gabriel García Márquez</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> This novel alone accumulated enough awards to place the author among the leading figures of the <a href="/wiki/Latin_American_Boom" title="Latin American Boom">Latin American Boom</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker6_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Booker6-35">[35]</a></sup> Some critics still consider <i>The Green House</i> to be Vargas Llosa's finest and most important achievement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker6_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Booker6-35">[35]</a></sup> Indeed, Latin American literary critic <a href="/wiki/Gerald_Martin" title="Gerald Martin">Gerald Martin</a> suggests that <i>The Green House</i> is "one of the greatest novels to have emerged from Latin America".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker6_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Booker6-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vargas Llosa's third novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Conversation_in_the_Cathedral" title="Conversation in the Cathedral">Conversation in the Cathedral</a></i> (<i>Conversación en la catedral</i>), was published in 1969, when he was 33. This ambitious narrative is the story of Santiago Zavala, the son of a government minister, and Ambrosio, his chauffeur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> A random meeting at a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dog_pound" title="Dog pound">dog pound</a> leads the pair to a riveting conversation at a nearby bar known as "The Cathedral".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> During the encounter, Zavala searches for the truth about his father's role in the murder of a notorious Peruvian underworld figure, shedding light on the workings of a dictatorship along the way. Unfortunately for Zavala, his quest results in a dead end with no answers and no sign of a better future.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro106_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Castro106-38">[38]</a></sup> The novel attacks the dictatorial government of Odría by showing how a dictatorship controls and destroys lives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cevallos273_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cevallos273-31">[31]</a></sup> The persistent theme of hopelessness makes <i>Conversation in the Cathedral</i> Vargas Llosa's most bitter novel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Castro106_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Castro106-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>He lectured Spanish American Literature at <a href="/wiki/King%27s_College_London" title="King's College London">King's College London</a> from 1969 to 1970.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s_and_the_.22discovery_of_humor.22">1970s and the "discovery of humor"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mario_Vargas_Llosa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title='Edit section: 1970s and the "discovery of humor"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1971, Vargas Llosa published <i>García Márquez: Story of a Deicide</i> (<i>García Márquez: historia de un deicidio</i>), which was his doctoral thesis for the Complutense University of Madrid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shaw431_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw431-41">[41]</a></sup> Although Vargas Llosa wrote this book-length study about his then friend, the Colombian <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Literature" title="Nobel Prize in Literature">Nobel laureate</a> writer Gabriel García Márquez, they did not speak to each other again. In 1976, Vargas Llosa punched García Márquez in the face in <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a> at the <i><a href="/wiki/Palacio_de_Bellas_Artes" title="Palacio de Bellas Artes">Palacio de Bellas Artes</a></i>, ending the friendship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Neither writer had publicly stated the underlying reasons for the quarrel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> A photograph of García Márquez sporting a black eye was published in 2007, reigniting public interest in the feud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-44">[44]</a></sup> Despite the decades of silence, in 2007, Vargas Llosa agreed to allow part of his book to be used as the introduction to a 40th-anniversary edition of García Márquez's <i><a href="/wiki/One_Hundred_Years_of_Solitude" title="One Hundred Years of Solitude">One Hundred Years of Solitude</a></i>, which was re-released in Spain and throughout Latin America that year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> <i>Historia de un Deicidio</i> was also reissued in that year, as part of Vargas Llosa's complete works.</p>
<p>Following the monumental work <i>Conversation in the Cathedral</i>, Vargas Llosa's output shifted away from more serious themes such as politics and problems with society. Latin American literary scholar Raymond L. Williams describes this phase in his writing career as "the discovery of humor".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-QC273_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-QC273-46">[46]</a></sup> His first attempt at a satirical novel was <i>Captain Pantoja and the Special Service</i> (<i>Pantaleón y las visitadoras</i>), published in 1973.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> This short, comic novel offers vignettes of dialogues and documents about the Peruvian armed forces and a corps of prostitutes assigned to visit military outposts in remote jungle areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker33_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Booker33-48">[48]</a></sup> These plot elements are similar to Vargas Llosa's earlier novel <i>The Green House</i>, but in a different form. As such, <i>Captain Pantoja and the Special Service</i> is essentially a <a href="/wiki/Parody" title="Parody">parody</a> of both <i>The Green House</i> and the literary approach that novel represents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker33_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Booker33-48">[48]</a></sup> Vargas Llosa's motivation to write the novel came from actually witnessing prostitutes being hired by the Peruvian Army and brought to serve soldiers in the jungle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>From 1974 to 1987, Vargas Llosa focused on his writing, but also took the time to pursue other endeavors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams60_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams60-50">[50]</a></sup> In 1975, he co-directed an unsuccessful motion-picture adaptation of his novel, <i>Captain Pantoja and the Secret Service</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams60_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams60-50">[50]</a></sup> In 1976 he was elected President of <a href="/wiki/PEN_International" title="PEN International">PEN International</a>, the worldwide association of writers and oldest human rights organisation, a position he held until 1979.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams60_50-2"><a href="#cite_note-Williams60-50">[50]</a></sup> During this time, Vargas Llosa constantly traveled to speak at conferences organized by internationally renowned institutions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_University_of_Jerusalem" title="Hebrew University of Jerusalem">Hebrew University of Jerusalem</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>, where he was Simón Bolívar Professor and an Overseas Fellow of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Churchill_College" title="Churchill College">Churchill College</a> in 1977–78.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1977, Vargas Llosa was elected as a member of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academia_Peruana_de_la_Lengua" title="Academia Peruana de la Lengua">Peruvian Academy of Language</a>, a membership he still holds today. That year, he also published <i><a href="/wiki/Aunt_Julia_and_the_Scriptwriter" title="Aunt Julia and the Scriptwriter">Aunt Julia and the Scriptwriter</a></i> (<i>La tía Julia y el escribidor</i>), based in part on his marriage to his first wife, Julia Urquidi, to whom he dedicated the novel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> She later wrote a memoir, <i>Lo que Varguitas no dijo</i> (<i>What Little Vargas Didn't Say</i>), in which she gives her personal account of their relationship. She states that Vargas Llosa's account exaggerates many negative points in their courtship and marriage while minimizing her role of assisting his literary career.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> <i>Aunt Julia and the Scriptwriter</i> is considered one of the most striking examples of how the language and imagery of popular culture can be used in literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> The novel was adapted in 1990 into a Hollywood feature film, <i><a href="/wiki/Tune_in_Tomorrow" title="Tune in Tomorrow">Tune in Tomorrow</a></i>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_novels">Later novels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mario_Vargas_Llosa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Later novels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mariovargasllosa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="483" data-file-width="538" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Mariovargasllosa.jpg/220px-Mariovargasllosa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Mariovargasllosa.jpg/330px-Mariovargasllosa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Mariovargasllosa.jpg/440px-Mariovargasllosa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mariovargasllosa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Vargas Llosa in 1982</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Vargas Llosa's fourth major novel, <i><a href="/wiki/The_War_of_the_End_of_the_World" title="The War of the End of the World">The War of the End of the World</a></i> (<i>La guerra del fin del mundo</i>), was published in 1981 and was his first attempt at a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical_novel" title="Historical novel">historical novel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal140_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal140-57">[57]</a></sup> This work initiated a radical change in Vargas Llosa's style towards themes such as <a href="/wiki/Messianism" title="Messianism">messianism</a> and irrational human behaviour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> It recreates the <a href="/wiki/War_of_Canudos" title="War of Canudos">War of Canudos</a>, an incident in 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> in which an armed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millenarian" title="Millenarian">millenarian</a> cult held off a siege by the national army for months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker75_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Booker75-59">[59]</a></sup> As in Vargas Llosa's earliest work, this novel carries a sober and serious theme, and its tone is dark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker75_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Booker75-59">[59]</a></sup> Vargas Llosa's bold exploration of humanity's propensity to idealize violence, and his account of a man-made catastrophe brought on by fanaticism on all sides, earned the novel substantial recognition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal124_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal124-60">[60]</a></sup> Because of the book's ambition and execution, critics have argued that this is one of Vargas Llosa's greatest literary pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal124_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal124-60">[60]</a></sup> Even though the novel has been acclaimed in Brazil, it was initially poorly received because a foreigner was writing about a Brazilian theme.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The book was also criticized as revolutionary and anti-socialist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Setti42_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Setti42-62">[62]</a></sup> Vargas Llosa says that this book is his favorite and was his most difficult accomplishment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Setti42_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Setti42-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>After completing <i>The War of the End of the World</i>, Vargas Llosa began to write novels that were significantly shorter than many of his earlier books. In 1983, he finished <i>The Real Life of Alejandro Mayta</i> (<i>Historia de Mayta</i>, 1984).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal140_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal140-57">[57]</a></sup> The novel focuses on a leftist insurrection that took place on May 29, 1962 in the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andean</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Jauja" title="Jauja">Jauja</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal140_57-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal140-57">[57]</a></sup> Later the same year, during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sendero_Luminoso" title="Sendero Luminoso">Sendero Luminoso</a> uprising, Vargas Llosa was asked by the Peruvian President <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Bela%C3%BAnde_Terry" title="Fernando Belaúnde Terry">Fernando Belaúnde Terry</a> to join the Investigatory Commission, a task force to inquire into the massacre of eight journalists at the hands of the villagers of <a href="/wiki/Uchuraccay" title="Uchuraccay">Uchuraccay</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The Commission's main purpose was to investigate the murders in order to provide information regarding the incident to the public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal151_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal151-64">[64]</a></sup> Following his involvement with the Investigatory Commission, Vargas Llosa published a series of articles to defend his position in the affair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal151_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal151-64">[64]</a></sup> In 1986, he completed his next novel, <i>Who Killed Palomino Molero</i> (<i>¿Quién mató a Palomino Molero?</i>), which he began writing shortly after the end of the Uchuraccay investigation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal151_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal151-64">[64]</a></sup> Though the plot of this <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mystery_(fiction)" title="Mystery (fiction)">mystery novel</a> is similar to the tragic events at Uchuraccay, literary critic Roy Boland points out that it was not an attempt to reconstruct the murders, but rather a "literary exorcism" of Vargas Llosa's own experiences during the commission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> The experience also inspired one of Vargas Llosa's later novels, <i><a href="/wiki/Death_in_the_Andes" title="Death in the Andes">Death in the Andes</a></i> (<i>Lituma en los Andes</i>), originally published in 1993 in Barcelona.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-franco56_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-franco56-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>It would be almost 20 years before Vargas Llosa wrote another major work: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Feast_of_the_Goat" title="The Feast of the Goat">The Feast of the Goat</a></i> (<i>La fiesta del chivo</i>), a <a href="/wiki/Political_thriller" title="Political thriller">political thriller</a>, was published in 2000 (and in English in 2001). According to Williams, it is Vargas Llosa's most complete and most ambitious novel since <i>The War of the End of the World</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams267_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams267-67">[67]</a></sup> Critic Sabine Koellmann sees it in the line of his earlier novels such as "Conversación en la catedral" depicting the effects of authoritarianism, violence and the abuse of power on the individual.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Koellmann239ff_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Koellmann239ff-68">[68]</a></sup> Based on the dictatorship of <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Trujillo" title="Rafael Trujillo">Rafael Trujillo</a>, who governed the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a> from 1930 until his assassination in 1961, the novel has three main strands: one concerns Urania Cabral, the daughter of a former politician and Trujillo loyalist, who returns for the first time since leaving the Dominican Republic after Trujillo's assassination 30 years earlier; the second concentrates on the assassination itself, the conspirators who carry it out, and its consequences; and the third and final strand deals with Trujillo himself in scenes from the end of his regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams267_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Williams267-67">[67]</a></sup> The book quickly received positive reviews in Spain and Latin America,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> and has had a significant impact in Latin America, being regarded as one of Vargas Llosa's best works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams267_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Williams267-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2003 he wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Way_to_Paradise" title="The Way to Paradise">The Way to Paradise</a></i> in which he studies <a href="/wiki/Flora_Tristan" title="Flora Tristan">Flora Tristan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gauguin" title="Paul Gauguin">Paul Gauguin</a>.</p>
<p>In 2006, Vargas Llosa wrote <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bad_Girl" title="The Bad Girl">The Bad Girl</a></i> (<i>Travesuras de la niña mala</i>), which journalist Kathryn Harrison argues is a rewrite (rather than simply a recycling) of <a href="/wiki/Gustave_Flaubert" title="Gustave Flaubert">Gustave Flaubert</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Madame_Bovary" title="Madame Bovary">Madame Bovary</a></i> (1856).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In Vargas Llosa's version, the plot relates the decades-long obsession of its narrator, a Peruvian expatriate in Paris, with a woman with whom he first fell in love when both were teenagers.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life_and_political_involvement">Later life and political involvement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mario_Vargas_Llosa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Later life and political involvement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sabatovargasllosa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="414" data-file-width="727" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Sabatovargasllosa.jpg/300px-Sabatovargasllosa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Sabatovargasllosa.jpg/450px-Sabatovargasllosa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Sabatovargasllosa.jpg/600px-Sabatovargasllosa.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sabatovargasllosa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Argentine writer <a href="/wiki/Ernesto_Sabato" title="Ernesto Sabato">Ernesto Sabato</a> (left) with Mario Vargas Llosa (right) in 1981</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Like many other Latin American intellectuals, Vargas Llosa was initially a supporter of the <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Revolution" title="Cuban Revolution">Cuban revolutionary</a> government of <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal_xi_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal_xi-33">[33]</a></sup> He studied <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a> in depth as a university student and was later persuaded by <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communist</a> ideals after the success of the Cuban Revolution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Gradually, Vargas Llosa came to believe that Cuban <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a> was incompatible with what he considered to be general liberties and freedoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> The official rupture between the writer and the policies of the Cuban government occurred with the so-called 'Padilla Affair', when the Castro regime imprisoned the poet <a href="/wiki/Heberto_Padilla" title="Heberto Padilla">Heberto Padilla</a> for a month in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Vargas Llosa, along with other intellectuals of the time, wrote to Castro protesting the Cuban political system and its imprisonment of the artist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Vargas Llosa has identified himself with liberalism rather than extreme left-wing political ideologies ever since.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Since he relinquished his earlier leftism, he has opposed both left- and right-wing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Authoritarian" title="Authoritarian">authoritarian</a> regimes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>With his appointment to the Investigatory Commission on the Uchuraccay massacre in 1983, he experienced what literary critic <a href="/wiki/Jean_Franco" title="Jean Franco">Jean Franco</a> calls "the most uncomfortable event in [his] political career".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-franco56_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-franco56-66">[66]</a></sup> Unfortunately for Vargas Llosa, his involvement with the Investigatory Commission led to immediate negative reactions and defamation from the Peruvian press; many suggested that the massacre was a conspiracy to keep the journalists from reporting the presence of government paramilitary forces in Uchuraccay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal151_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal151-64">[64]</a></sup> The commission concluded that it was the indigenous villagers who had been responsible for the killings; for Vargas Llosa the incident showed "how vulnerable democracy is in Latin America and how easily it dies under dictatorships of the right and left".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> These conclusions, and Vargas Llosa personally, came under intense criticism: anthropologist Enrique Mayer, for instance, accused him of "paternalism",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> while fellow anthropologist Carlos Iván Degregori criticized him for his ignorance of the Andean world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Vargas Llosa was accused of actively colluding in a government cover-up of army involvement in the massacre.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal151_64-4"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal151-64">[64]</a></sup> US <a href="/wiki/Latin_American_literature" title="Latin American literature">Latin American literature</a> scholar Misha Kokotovic summarizes that the novelist was charged with seeing "indigenous cultures as a 'primitive' obstacle to the full realization of his Western model of modernity".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Shocked both by the atrocity itself and then by the reaction his report had provoked, Vargas Llosa responded that his critics were apparently more concerned with his report than with the hundreds of peasants who would later die at the hands of the Sendero Luminoso guerrilla organization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Acto_fundacional_UPD3.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="300" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Acto_fundacional_UPD3.jpg/220px-Acto_fundacional_UPD3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d2/Acto_fundacional_UPD3.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Acto_fundacional_UPD3.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Vargas Llosa at the founding act of <a href="/wiki/Union,_Progress_and_Democracy" title="Union, Progress and Democracy">UPD</a>, September 2007</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Over the course of the decade, Vargas Llosa became known as a "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neoliberal" title="Neoliberal">neoliberal</a>", although he personally dislikes the term and considers it "pure nonsense" and only used for derision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-llosa2001global_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-llosa2001global-82">[82]</a></sup> In 1987, he helped form and soon became a leader of the <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Movement" title="Liberty Movement">Movimiento Libertad</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bolland8_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bolland8-83">[83]</a></sup> The following year his party entered a coalition with the parties of Peru's two principal conservative politicians at the time, ex-president <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Bela%C3%BAnde_Terry" title="Fernando Belaúnde Terry">Fernando Belaúnde Terry</a> (of the <a href="/wiki/Popular_Action_(Peru)" title="Popular Action (Peru)">Popular Action</a> party) and <a href="/wiki/Luis_Bedoya_Reyes" title="Luis Bedoya Reyes">Luis Bedoya Reyes</a> (of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partido_Popular_Cristiano_(Peru)" title="Partido Popular Cristiano (Peru)">Partido Popular Cristiano</a></i>), to form the tripartite center-right coalition known as <i><a href="/wiki/Democratic_Front_(Peru)" title="Democratic Front (Peru)">Frente Democrático</a></i> (FREDEMO).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bolland8_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bolland8-83">[83]</a></sup> He ran for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Presidents_of_Peru" title="List of Presidents of Peru">presidency of Peru</a> in 1990 as the candidate of the FREDEMO coalition. He proposed a drastic economic <a href="/wiki/Austerity" title="Austerity">austerity</a> program that frightened most of the country's poor; this program emphasized the need for privatization, a market economy, free trade, and most importantly, the dissemination of private property.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emilyparker_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-emilyparker-84">[84]</a></sup> Although he won the first round with 34% of the vote, Vargas Llosa was defeated by a then-unknown agricultural engineer, <a href="/wiki/Alberto_Fujimori" title="Alberto Fujimori">Alberto Fujimori</a>, in the subsequent run-off.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emilyparker_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-emilyparker-84">[84]</a></sup> Vargas Llosa included an account of his run for the presidency in the memoir <i><a href="/wiki/A_Fish_in_the_Water" title="A Fish in the Water">A Fish in the Water</a></i> (<i>El pez en el agua</i>, 1993).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Since his political defeat, he has focused mainly on his writing, with only occasional political involvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>A month after losing the election, at the invitation of <a href="/wiki/Octavio_Paz" title="Octavio Paz">Octavio Paz</a>, Vargas Llosa attended a conference in Mexico entitled, "The 20th Century: The Experience of Freedom". Focused on the collapse of communist rule in central and eastern Europe, it was broadcast on Mexican television from 27 August to 2 September. Addressing the conference on 30 August 1990, Vargas Llosa embarrassed his hosts by condemning the Mexican system of power based on the rule of the <a href="/wiki/Institutional_Revolutionary_Party" title="Institutional Revolutionary Party">Institutional Revolutionary Party</a> (PRI), which had been in power for 61 years. Criticizing the PRI by name, he commented, "I don't believe that there has been in Latin America any case of a system of dictatorship which has so efficiently recruited the intellectual milieu, bribing it with great subtlety." He declared, "Mexico is the perfect dictatorship. The perfect dictatorship is not communism, not the USSR, not Fidel Castro; the perfect dictatorship is Mexico. Because it is a camouflaged dictatorship."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-terra_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-terra-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The statement, "Mexico is the perfect dictatorship" became a cliché in Mexico<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEReding1991257_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEReding1991257-88">[88]</a></sup> and internationally, until the PRI fell from power in 2000.</p>
<p>Vargas Llosa has mainly lived in Madrid since the 1990s, but spends roughly three months of the year in Peru with his extended family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emilyparker_84-2"><a href="#cite_note-emilyparker-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> He also frequently visits London where he occasionally spends long periods. Vargas Llosa acquired Spanish citizenship in 1993, though he still holds <a href="/wiki/Peruvian_nationality_law" title="Peruvian nationality law">Peruvian nationality</a>. The writer often reiterates his love for both countries. In his Nobel speech he observed: "I carry Peru deep inside me because that is where I was born, grew up, was formed, and lived those experiences of childhood and youth that shaped my personality and forged my calling". He then added: "I love Spain as much as Peru, and my debt to her is as great as my gratitude. If not for Spain, I never would have reached this podium or become a known writer".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1994 he was elected a member of the <i><a href="/wiki/Real_Academia_Espa%C3%B1ola" title="Real Academia Española">Real Academia Española</a></i> (Royal Spanish Academy), he took up seat L on 15 January 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams83_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams83-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> He has been involved in the country's political arena. In February 2008 he stopped supporting the <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Party_(Spain)" title="People's Party (Spain)">People's Party</a> in favor of the recently created <a href="/wiki/Union,_Progress_and_Democracy" title="Union, Progress and Democracy">Union, Progress and Democracy</a>, claiming that certain conservative views held by the former party are at odds with his classical liberal beliefs. His political ideologies appear in the book <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Pol%C3%ADtica_razonable&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Política razonable (page does not exist)">Política razonable</a></i>, written with <a href="/wiki/Fernando_Savater" title="Fernando Savater">Fernando Savater</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rosa_D%C3%ADez" title="Rosa Díez">Rosa Díez</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_Pombo" title="Álvaro Pombo">Álvaro Pombo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albert_Boadella" title="Albert Boadella">Albert Boadella</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Mart%C3%ADnez_Gorriar%C3%A1n" title="Carlos Martínez Gorriarán">Carlos Martínez Gorriarán</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> He continues to write, both journalism and fiction, and to travel extensively. He has also taught as a visiting professor at a number of prominent universities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 18, 2010, Vargas Llosa received the honorary degree Degree of Letters from the <a href="/wiki/City_College_of_New_York" title="City College of New York">City College of New York</a> of the <a href="/wiki/City_University_of_New_York" title="City University of New York">City University of New York</a>, where he also delivered the President's Lecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 4 February 2011, Vargas Llosa was raised into the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_nobility" title="Spanish nobility">Spanish nobility</a> by <a href="/wiki/Juan_Carlos_I_of_Spain" title="Juan Carlos I of Spain">King Juan Carlos I</a> with the hereditary title of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marquessate_of_Vargas_Llosa" title="Marquessate of Vargas Llosa">Marqués de Vargas Llosa</a></i> (English: Marquis of Vargas Llosa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2011, the writer took part in the <a href="/wiki/Peruvian_general_election,_2011" title="Peruvian general election, 2011">Peruvian general election, 2011</a> by saying he was going to vote for <a href="/wiki/Alejandro_Toledo" title="Alejandro Toledo">Alejandro Toledo</a> (Peruvian former president 2001–2006). After casting his vote, he said his country should stay in the path of legality and freedom.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Vargas Llosa joined the <a href="/wiki/Mont_Pelerin_Society" title="Mont Pelerin Society">Mont Pelerin Society</a> in 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-forbesmontpelerin_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-forbesmontpelerin-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>As for hobbies, Vargas Llosa is very fond of <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">association football</a>, and is a renowned supporter of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universitario_de_Deportes" title="Universitario de Deportes">Universitario de Deportes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> The writer himself has confessed in his book <i><a href="/wiki/A_Fish_in_the_Water" title="A Fish in the Water">A Fish in the Water</a></i> since childhood he has been a fan of the 'cream colored' team from <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>, which was first seen in the field one day in 1946 when he was only 10 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> In February 2011, Vargas Llosa was awarded with an honorary life membership of this football club, in a ceremony which took place in the <a href="/wiki/Estadio_Monumental_%22U%22" title='Estadio Monumental "U"'>Monumental Stadium</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lima" title="Lima">Lima</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>He was named in the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Papers" title="Panama Papers">Panama Papers</a> released by the <a href="/wiki/International_Consortium_of_Investigative_Journalists" title="International Consortium of Investigative Journalists">International Consortium of Investigative Journalists</a> on 10 May 2016.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Style_of_writing">Style of writing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mario_Vargas_Llosa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Style of writing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plot.2C_setting.2C_and_major_themes">Plot, setting, and major themes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mario_Vargas_Llosa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Plot, setting, and major themes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Vargas Llosa's style encompasses historical material as well as his own personal experiences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> For example, in his first novel, <i>The Time of the Hero</i>, his own experiences at the Leoncio Prado military school informed his depiction of the corrupt social institution which mocked the moral standards it was supposed to uphold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal32_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal32-30">[30]</a></sup> Furthermore, the corruption of the book's school is a reflection of the corruption of Peruvian society at the time the novel was written.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kristal33_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kristal33-32">[32]</a></sup> Vargas Llosa frequently uses his writing to challenge the inadequacies of society, such as demoralization and oppression by those in political power towards those who challenge this power. One of the main themes he has explored in his writing is the individual's struggle for freedom within an oppressive reality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> For example, his two-volume novel <i>Conversation in the Cathedral</i> is based on the tyrannical dictatorship of Peruvian President <a href="/wiki/Manuel_A._Odr%C3%ADa" title="Manuel A. Odría">Manuel A. Odría</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> The protagonist, Santiago, rebels against the suffocating dictatorship by participating in the subversive activities of leftist political groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> In addition to themes such as corruption and oppression, Vargas Llosa's second novel, <i>The Green House</i>, explores "a denunciation of Peru's basic institutions", dealing with issues of abuse and exploitation of the workers in the brothel by corrupt military officers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-QC273_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-QC273-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many of Vargas Llosa's earlier novels were set in Peru, while in more recent work he has expanded to other regions of Latin America, such as Brazil and the Dominican Republic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-castro-klaren19_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-castro-klaren19-109">[109]</a></sup> His responsibilities as a writer and lecturer have allowed him to travel frequently and led to settings for his novels in regions outside of Peru.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams60_50-3"><a href="#cite_note-Williams60-50">[50]</a></sup> <i>The War of the End of the World</i> was his first major work set outside Peru.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cevallos273_31-4"><a href="#cite_note-Cevallos273-31">[31]</a></sup> Though the plot deals with historical events of the <a href="/wiki/Canudos" title="Canudos">Canudos</a> revolt against the Brazilian government, the novel is not based directly on historical fact; rather, its main inspiration is the non-fiction account of those events published by Brazilian writer <a href="/wiki/Euclides_da_Cunha" title="Euclides da Cunha">Euclides da Cunha</a> in 1902.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker75_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Booker75-59">[59]</a></sup> <i>The Feast of the Goat</i>, based on the dictatorship of <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Trujillo" title="Rafael Trujillo">Rafael Trujillo</a>, takes place in the <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Republic" title="Dominican Republic">Dominican Republic</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams267_67-3"><a href="#cite_note-Williams267-67">[67]</a></sup> in preparation for this novel, Vargas Llosa undertook a comprehensive study of Dominican history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams270_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams270-110">[110]</a></sup> The novel was characteristically <a href="/wiki/Literary_realism" title="Literary realism">realist</a>, and Vargas Llosa underscores that he "respected the basic facts, ... I have not exaggerated", but at the same time he points out "It's a novel, not a history book, so I took many, many liberties."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of Vargas Llosa's more recent novels, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Way_to_Paradise" title="The Way to Paradise">The Way to Paradise</a></i> (<i>El paraíso en la otra esquina</i>), is set largely in France and <a href="/wiki/Tahiti" title="Tahiti">Tahiti</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> Based on the biography of former social reformer <a href="/wiki/Flora_Tristan" title="Flora Tristan">Flora Tristan</a>, it demonstrates how Flora and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Gauguin" title="Paul Gauguin">Paul Gauguin</a> were unable to find paradise, but were still able to inspire followers to keep working towards a socialist <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Unfortunately, Vargas Llosa was not as successful in transforming these historical figures into fiction. Some critics, such as Barbara Mujica, argue that <i>The Way to Paradise</i> lacks the "audacity, energy, political vision, and narrative genius" that was present in his previous works.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modernism_and_postmodernism">Modernism and postmodernism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Mario_Vargas_Llosa&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Modernism and postmodernism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The works of Mario Vargas Llosa are viewed as both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modernist" title="Modernist">modernist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Postmodernist" title="Postmodernist">postmodernist</a> novels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker32_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Booker32-115">[115]</a></sup> Though there is still much debate over the differences between modernist and postmodernist literature, literary scholar M. Keith Booker claims that the difficulty and technical complexity of Vargas Llosa's early works, such as <i>The Green House</i> and <i>Conversation in the Cathedral</i>, are clearly elements of the modern novel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker6_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-Booker6-35">[35]</a></sup> Furthermore, these earlier novels all carry a certain seriousness of attitude—another important defining aspect of modernist art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker32_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Booker32-115">[115]</a></sup> By contrast, his later novels such as <i>Captain Pantoja and the Special Service</i>, <i>Aunt Julia and the Scriptwriter</i>, <i>The Real Life of Alejandro Mayta</i>, and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Storyteller_(novel)" title="The Storyteller (novel)">The Storyteller</a></i> (<i>El hablador</i>) appear to follow a postmodernist mode of writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> These novels have a much lighter, <a href="/wiki/Farce" title="Farce">farcical</a>, and comic tone, characteristics of postmodernism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker33_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-Booker33-48">[48]</a></sup> Comparing two of Vargas Llosa's novels, <i>The Green House</i> and <i>Captain Pantoja and the Special Service</i>, Booker discusses the contrast between modernism and postmodernism found in the writer's works: while both novels explore the theme of prostitution as well as the workings of the Peruvian military, Booker points out that the former is gravely serious whereas the latter is ridiculously comic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booker33_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-Booker33-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MarioVargasLlosaAitanaSanchezGijon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1456" data-file-width="2592" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/MarioVargasLlosaAitanaSanchezGijon.jpg/400px-MarioVargasLlosaAitanaSanchezGijon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/MarioVargasLlosaAitanaSanchezGijon.jpg/600px-MarioVargasLlosaAitanaSanchezGijon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/MarioVargasLlosaAitanaSanchezGijon.jpg/800px-MarioVargasLlosaAitanaSanchezGijon.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a>
